    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="72abe86734894962bf3e8609864de0a3">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'a339ba2c-cd53-4c5b-86dc-d7d9db4e4562'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <h1 class="citation__title" lang="en">High Capacity and Fast Kinetics Enabled by Metal-Doping in Prussian Blue Analogue Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</h1>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d51652681" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Prussian blue analogues (PBAs) have gained tremendous attention as promising low-cost electrochemically-tunable electrode materials, which can accommodate large Na<sup>+</sup> ions with attractive specific capacity and charge-discharge kinetics. However, poor cycling stability caused by lattice strain and volume change remains to be improved. Herein, metal-doping strategy has been demonstrated in FeNiHCF, Na<sub>1.40</sub>Fe<sub>0.90</sub>Ni<sub>0.10</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.85</sub> ⋅ 1.3H<sub>2</sub>O, delivering a capacity as high as 148 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. At an exceptionally high rate of 25.6 A g<sup>−1</sup>, a reversible capacity of ~55 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> still can be obtained with a very small capacity decay rate of 0.02 % per cycle for 1000 cycles, considered one of the best among all metal-doped PBAs. This exhibits the stabilizing effect of Ni doping which enhances structural stability and long-term cyclability. <i>In situ</i> synchrotron X-ray diffraction reveals an extremely small (~1 %) change in unit cell parameters. The Ni substitution is found to increase the electronic conductivity and redox activity, especially at the low-spin (LS) Fe center due to inductive effect. This larger capacity contribution from LS Fe<sup>2+</sup>C<sub>6</sub>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>C<sub>6</sub> redox couple is responsible for stable high-rate capability of FeNiHCF. The insight gained in this work may pave the way for the design of other high-performance electrode materials for sustainable sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="asia202301145-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="asia202301145-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The search for better energy storage technologies has been the main focus in recent years due to the growing need for large-scale energy storage especially for renewable energy smart grids.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0001_R_d51652459e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> The limitations of the currently available lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), such as lithium natural reserves/supplies, non-renewability and high toxicity of transition metals used in electrodes, high cost, and limited energy density which is becoming more challenging to increase, have driven the trend of post-LIBs with more sustainable electrode materials.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0004_R_d51652459e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> Sodium-based batteries have gained much attention due to their eco-friendliness as well as the inexhaustible resources and lower price of sodium. Despite these advantages, the development of cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) has been dominated by inorganic compounds, such as layered metal oxides and polyanions, which usually show poor rate capability, specific capacity, and cycling stability.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0005_R_d51652459e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2a - Nat. Energy">2a</a>, <a href="#asia202301145-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0008_R_d51652459e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Storage Mater.">3</a></sup></span> This is mainly due to the significantly larger size of Na<sup>+</sup> ion (1.02 Å vs. 0.76 Å for Li<sup>+</sup>) which unavoidably affects the transport kinetics of ion insertion/extraction processes and drastically induces lattice distortion, large volume change, and phase transition of rigid inorganic host lattice.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0008_R_d51652459e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Storage Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#asia202301145-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0009_R_d51652459e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - J. Mater. Chem. A">4</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>As a fascinating family of compounds, hexacyanometallates or Prussian blue analogues (PBAs), with a general formula A<sub>2</sub>MM′(CN)<sub>6</sub> structurally closely related to that of face-centered cubic perovskites ABX<sub>3</sub>, are known for chemical and redox tunability which are deemed useful for battery applications.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0010_R_d51652459e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span> Various transition metals M and M′ (such as Fe, Mn, Ni, Co, V, and Cu) in the B sites occupying alternate corners of the cube are linked together by linear cyanide (C≡N<sup>−</sup>) ligands instead of the oxygen ions (Figure <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0001">1a</a>). As a result, M which is octahedrally coordinated only with the nitrogen atoms of the C≡N<sup>−</sup> bridging ligands (high-spin), and M′ which has octahedral coordination only with the carbon atoms of the cyanide groups (low-spin), are allowed to be more separated, therefore opening up the three-dimensional robust structure for guest-ion insertion (Figure <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0001">1a</a>).<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0011_R_d51652459e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a></sup></span> The large A sites (~4.6 Å in diameter) usually contain mobile alkali metal ions which can transport rapidly via open channels in the ⟨100⟩ directions (~3.2 Å in diameter).<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0014_R_d51652459e566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span> The ion diffusion coefficient of PBAs can be as high as 10<sup>−9</sup> to 10<sup>−8</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> in some conditions which is much higher than ionic conduction in the conventional insertion oxide and phosphate cathodes.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0010_R_d51652459e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span> PBAs usually also contain some M′(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancies as large as 5 Å (with an actual PBA chemical formula of A<sub>2-<i>x</i></sub>M[M′(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>1-<i>y</i></sub>□<sub><i>y</i></sub> ⋅ <i>z</i>H<sub>2</sub>O, where 0≤x≤2, 0≤y &lt;1, and □=M′(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancies).<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0015_R_d51652459e604" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Mater. Today Commun.">8</a></sup></span> Water molecules with a total amount denoted by <i>z</i> may reside in the empty A and B sites, known as zeolitic water and coordinated water (coordinated to the M ions), respectively.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0010_R_d51652459e609" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a>, <a href="#asia202301145-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0016_R_d51652459e609" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Power Sources">9</a></sup></span> The degree of vacancies and the number of alkali ions primarily affect the oxidation states of the transition metals due to the electroneutrality of the PBA lattice; therefore, the electrochemical properties may be fine-tuned.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0010_R_d51652459e612" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="asia202301145-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d6890be1-6c3d-43d3-b9f5-569ac6f98475/asia202301145-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d6890be1-6c3d-43d3-b9f5-569ac6f98475/asia202301145-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/29db4182-ac85-4ab5-acb6-e85c55ae5724/asia202301145-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d6890be1-6c3d-43d3-b9f5-569ac6f98475/asia202301145-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=asia202301145-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fasia.202301145" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Ideal structure of a Prussian blue analogue A<sub>2</sub>MM′(CN)<sub>6</sub> without any M′(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancies, where M is high-spin (HS) transition metal octahedrally coordinated with nitrogen atoms of the C≡N<sup>−</sup> bridging ligands and M′ is low-spin (LS) metal which has octahedral coordination with carbon atoms of the ligands. A usually has partial occupancy and is mobile (Na<sup>+</sup> in this work). Water molecules are omitted for clarity. M<sup>HS</sup> is doped with Ni ions in this work. (b) PXRD patterns of FeNiHCF, NiHCF, and FeHCF. (c) SEM image of FeNiHCF. (d) and (e) FT-IR spectra and TGA data of FeNiHCF, NiHCF, and FeHCF.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>With the large open channels suitable for Na-ion insertion, robust three-dimensional framework, and electrochemical tunability, PBAs also offer simple synthesis procedures and low material costs. As a result, many research attempts have been made at fine-tuning the PBA properties in terms of capacity, redox potential, cycling stability, and rate capability, with the ultimate goal of establishing an ideal composition for ultra-high-performance rechargeable batteries.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0010_R_d51652459e654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a>, <a href="#asia202301145-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0014_R_d51652459e654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span> Among these aspects, cycling stability is considered to be a major issue, especially because of the large ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> which puts a lot of strain on the host lattice, causing volume change.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0017_R_d51652459e659" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Mater. Chem. A">10</a></sup></span> This consequently leads to unstable electrode/electrolyte interface, giving rise to continuous rupture and reconstruction of unstable solid electrolyte interphase, which in turn results in poor charge/discharge efficiency and long-term cyclability.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0010_R_d51652459e662" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a>, <a href="#asia202301145-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0017_R_d51652459e662" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Mater. Chem. A">10</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>To tackle this, metal-ion doping is one of the most effective modification methods.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0010_R_d51652459e668" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a>, <a href="#asia202301145-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0018_R_d51652459e668" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">11</a></sup></span> Redox-inactive Zn<sup>2+</sup> was used to partially substitute high-spin Fe (Fe<sup>HS</sup>) in Fe-based hexacyanoferrate (FeHCF, Na<sub>1.98</sub>Fe<sub>0.89</sub>Zn<sub>0.11</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.90</sub>, 0.11 Zn : 0.89 Fe<sup>HS</sup>), which was found to enhance the redox activity of both Fe<sup>HS</sup> and low-spin Fe (Fe<sup>LS</sup>) due to increased electronic conductivity and Na-ion migration. The Zn-substituted FeHCF delivered an initial capacity of 145 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a rate of 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and an initial capacity of 98.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for the highest rate reported of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0019_R_d51652459e701" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem. Eng. J.">12</a></sup></span> Electrochemically inert Cu<sup>2+</sup> was also utilized to dope FeHCF. Even though the exact concentration of Cu ions is unclear, the doped material was reported to have a small band gap and improved sodium storage kinetics, with an initial capacity of ~90 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and a reversible capacity of 47 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 3.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0020_R_d51652459e715" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Power Sources">13</a></sup></span> Another attempt at optimizing the performance of Cu-doped FeHCF was made by Chen and co-workers, with a doping concentration of 0.08 Cu : 0.92 Fe<sup>HS</sup>. The improved performance was found to arise from the enhanced electrochemical activity of Fe<sup>LS</sup> and the stabilizing effect of Cu on the PBA crystal structure. The highest initial capacity was reported at 127.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and an initial capacity of 82.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0018_R_d51652459e731" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">11</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Ni-based HCF (NiHCF) is known for its zero-strain characteristics during Na-ion insertion/extraction processes, i. e., lattice parameters barely change (&lt;1 %) during cycling.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0017_R_d51652459e736" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Mater. Chem. A">10</a></sup></span> As a result, capacity decay which primarily derives from structural deformation is eliminated, leading to better cycling stability and Coulombic efficiency. However, as reported by You et al., Fe<sup>LS</sup> in Na<sub>0.84</sub>Ni[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.71</sub> was the only metal center with redox activity; therefore, the initial capacity was quite limited (66 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup>).<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0017_R_d51652459e752" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Mater. Chem. A">10</a></sup></span> To take advantage of this feature, Ni<sup>2+</sup>, as an electrochemically inactive component, may be introduced into the lattice to alleviate the mechanical stress, buffer the volume change, and enhance structural stability during Na-ion intercalation.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0017_R_d51652459e757" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Mater. Chem. A">10</a></sup></span> Yang and co-workers investigated the effect of Ni substitution in Mn-based HCF Na<sub>1.80</sub>Mn[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.99</sub> with various Ni contents up to 0.76 Ni per formula unit. It was found that the best performance was obtained when the degree of doping was 0.12 Cu : 0.88 Mn<sup>HS</sup> with an initial capacity of 123.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. However, due to the redox activity of Jahn-Teller Mn<sup>2+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> couple,<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0010_R_d51652459e778" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span> Ni can only help stabilize the structure and lattice distortion to a certain extent, with a capacity retention of 83.8 % at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> after 800 cycles.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0021_R_d51652459e783" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Chem. Commun.">14</a></sup></span> Quan and co-workers investigated a Ni-doped Co-based HCF with a ratio of 0.17 Ni : 0.83 Co<sup>HS</sup>, which delivered 146 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and much better capacity retention at different rates (up to 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>), compared to the undoped material. This was explained by the abundant d-orbital electrons of Ni which improved the electronic state structure and conductivity, thus reducing the accumulation of electrons on the material surface.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0022_R_d51652459e795" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">15</a></sup></span> Fu et al. reported optimized Na storage performance of 3 %-Ni-doped FeHCF with an initial capacity of 117 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The Ni doping allowed the capacity contribution from Fe<sup>LS</sup> to be as high as 50 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and alleviated the structural instability at high rates, which is known to be problematic in many PBA-based SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0023_R_d51652459e807" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Mater. Chem. A">16</a></sup></span> The high rate capability of the Ni-doped material was found to be mainly contributed by the Fe<sup>LS</sup> redox couple. Therefore, activating Fe<sup>LS</sup> by Ni doping has great potential to improve the rate performance. However, due to the zeolitic water and unknown Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancies, the highest rate reported was only 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup> with a reversible capacity of ~50 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0023_R_d51652459e821" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Mater. Chem. A">16</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>In light of these findings, we have synthesized highly crystallized sodium iron-nickel hexacyanoferrate (FeNiHCF, Na<sub>1.40</sub>Fe<sub>0.90</sub>Ni<sub>0.10</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.85</sub> ⋅ 1.3H<sub>2</sub>O), by using a modified co-precipitation method assisted with sodium citrate as a chelating agent. With this Ni-doping concentration and a relatively low degree of vacancies and water content, FeNiHCF has been found to deliver superior electrochemical performance in SIBs, in comparison with FeHCF and NiHCF investigated in this work. The presence of redox-inactive Ni<sup>2+</sup> in the structure enhances the redox activity of both Fe<sup>HS</sup> and Fe<sup>LS</sup> as well as the electronic conductivity, resulting in FeNiHCF delivering an initial capacity as high as 148 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. At an extremely high rate of 25.6 A g<sup>−1</sup>, a reversible capacity of ~55 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> still can be obtained with a very small capacity decay rate of 0.02 % per cycle for 1000 cycles. This highlights the stabilizing effect of Ni ions, leading to better structural stability and long-term cyclability especially at high rates. The detailed mechanistic studies and insight gained in this work may pave the way for the design of other high-performance electrode materials from this low-cost robust family of PBA compounds.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="asia202301145-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="asia202301145-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Ni-doped FeHCF (FeNiHCF) was synthesized by a modified co-precipitation method, with sodium citrate as a chelating agent (details in the Supporting Information).<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0015_R_d51652459e863" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Mater. Today Commun.">8</a></sup></span> In PBA synthesis, the nucleation and crystal growth process usually have very fast reaction kinetics due to extremely small solubility product constants (<i>K<sub>sp</sub></i>), leading to irregularly-shaped nanosized products.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0024_R_d51652459e870" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a></sup></span> The co-precipitation method was chosen to better control the rate of nucleation and crystal growth as well as the crystallinity of PBA materials synthesized.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0025_R_d51652459e873" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a></sup></span> The sodium citrateadded increases the concentration of Na ions in the reaction and helps slow down the crystallization process. This is achieved by citrate ions chelating with the metal ions to form metal-citrate chelate complexes, which can act as a reservoir to slowly release metal cations into the reaction mixture, in order to react with hexacyanoferrate Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub><sup>3−/4−</sup> anions and subsequently form nuclei for the crystal growth process.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0024_R_d51652459e880" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a></sup></span> The citrate anions may also absorb on the surface of the nuclei, thus suppressing the growth rate and inhibiting crystal aggregation.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0024_R_d51652459e883" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0001">1b</a> shows the powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD) pattern of FeNiHCF, indicating a pure crystalline sample in comparison with those of FeHCF and NiHCF, which were also prepared by a similar synthesis procedure. The profile refinement of the PXRD data in Figure S1 confirms cubic space group <i>F</i>m<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/7cafea0b-79b7-41c8-9ea7-72f29fec5735/asia202301145-math-0001.png"></span><math></math>
m with unit cell parameter a=10.30 Å for FeNiHCF.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0023_R_d51652459e906" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Mater. Chem. A">16</a></sup></span> It is worth noting that Ni substitution leads to lattice expansion (unit cell parameter a=10.22 Å for FeHCF to a=10.30 Å for FeNiHCF), due to a larger size of Ni ion compared to that of Fe. These parameters are consistent with those reported elsewhere.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0021_R_d51652459e909" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Chem. Commun.">14</a>, <a href="#asia202301145-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0023_R_d51652459e909" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Mater. Chem. A">16</a>, <a href="#asia202301145-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0026_R_d51652459e909" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Power Sources">19</a></sup></span> Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image in Figure <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0001">1c</a> reveals the cubic morphology of FeNiHCF with a particle size in the range of ~200−1000 nm with no sign of any impurity. Fourier-transform infrared (FT-IR) spectra in Figure <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0001">1d</a> show a strong peak at 2091 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which corresponds to the stretching vibration of the C≡N<sup>−</sup> group, and a peak at approximately 592 cm<sup>−1</sup> attributed to the bending mode of Fe−CN.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0024_R_d51652459e925" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a>, <a href="#asia202301145-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0027_R_d51652459e925" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">20</a></sup></span> In addition, the absorption peaks at 3547 and 1620 cm<sup>−1</sup> can be assigned to O−H stretching and H−O−H bending modes of water, indicating the presence of water molecules at the vacancies and A sites.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0024_R_d51652459e931" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a>, <a href="#asia202301145-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0028_R_d51652459e931" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">21</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was performed on the three compounds (Figure <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0001">1e</a>) to determine the water contents. The distinct weight loss below 280 °C of FeNiHCF curve can be assigned to the removal of adsorbed water and the elimination of interstitial water (8.2 %), while NiHCF and FeHCF show relatively larger losses.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0015_R_d51652459e939" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Mater. Today Commun.">8</a>, <a href="#asia202301145-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0017_R_d51652459e939" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Mater. Chem. A">10</a>, <a href="#asia202301145-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0028_R_d51652459e939" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">21</a></sup></span> Interestingly, the final weight loss step which corresponds to the decomposition of the framework takes place at a higher temperature for FeNiHCF (&gt;300 °C), compared with the other two materials.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0029_R_d51652459e942" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Energy">22</a></sup></span> This presumably suggests higher stability of the FeNiHCF structure under the testing conditions. Inductively coupled plasma-mass spectrometry (ICP-MS) and elemental analysis (EA), together with the TGA results (Table S1) were used to determine the chemical formulas of FeNiHCF, NiHCF, and FeHCF as Na<sub>1.40</sub>Fe<sub>0.90</sub>Ni<sub>0.10</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.85</sub> ⋅ 1.3H<sub>2</sub>O, Na<sub>1.56</sub>Ni[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.91</sub> ⋅ 2.1H<sub>2</sub>O, and Na<sub>1.17</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.79</sub> ⋅ 3.3H<sub>2</sub>O, respectively.</p>
         
         <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was used to determine the valence state of Ni and Fe in the as-synthesized compounds (Figure S2). The deconvolution of the high-resolution Ni 2p spectra of FeNiHCF and NiHCF shows the presence of Ni<sup>2+</sup>, with Ni 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Ni 2p<sub>1/2</sub> observed at 856.6 and 874.2 eV, respectively, and satellite peaks observed at 862.7 and 880.3 eV.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0022_R_d51652459e984" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">15</a>, <a href="#asia202301145-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0030_R_d51652459e984" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Chem. Eng. J.">23</a></sup></span> For high-resolution Fe 2p spectra, all three compounds FeNiHCF, FeHCF, and NiHCF show Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub> at 708.5 and 721.4 eV, respectively, corresponding to Fe<sup>2+</sup>, while NiHCF shows additional components at 712.0 and 724.6 eV attributed to Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of Fe<sup>3+</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0019_R_d51652459e1000" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem. Eng. J.">12</a>, <a href="#asia202301145-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0024_R_d51652459e1000" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a></sup></span> This is consistent with the charge balance of the NiHCF chemical formula which requires a small amount of Fe<sup>3+</sup> to obtain electroneutrality.</p>
         
         <p>FeNiHCF was tested as cathode material with Na metal as anode and 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in 1 : 1 v/v ethylene carbonate (EC)/propylene carbonate (PC) as electrolyte. Figure <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0002">2a</a> exhibits the cyclic voltammogram (CV) of FeNiHCF at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> in comparison with those of NiHCF and FeHCF in the voltage range of 2.0 to 4.2 V (vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). FeNiHCF shows two pairs of redox couples, 3.38/3.57 V and 2.80/3.09 V corresponding to Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> (LS) and Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> (HS), respectively.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0018_R_d51652459e1028" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">11</a>, <a href="#asia202301145-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0031_R_d51652459e1028" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">24</a></sup></span> The positions of the CV peaks are in good agreement with the voltage plateaus observed in the charge-discharge profiles in Figure <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0002">2b</a>. For LS Fe<sup>3+</sup>C<sub>6</sub> (d<sup>5</sup>) to undergo reduction to become Fe<sup>2+</sup>C<sub>6</sub> (d<sup>6</sup>) during discharge, one electron will be added to the t<sup>5</sup> configuration of LS Fe<sup>3+</sup>C<sub>6</sub> to completely fill up the t<sub>2g</sub> band, which is favored in energy.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0031_R_d51652459e1056" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">24</a></sup></span> In the case of HS Fe<sup>3+</sup>N<sub>6</sub> (d<sup>5</sup>), one electron will be added to the e<sup>2</sup>t<sup>3</sup> configuration forming Fe<sup>2+</sup>N<sub>6</sub> (d<sup>6</sup>), which gains no interatomic exchange stabilization and is considered unfavored in energy compared to the LS system.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0031_R_d51652459e1077" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">24</a></sup></span> As a result, discharging (or electron filling) of LS Fe<sup>3+</sup>C<sub>6</sub> is much more energetically favorable, leading to the redox couple of LS Fe<sup>2+</sup>C<sub>6</sub>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>C<sub>6</sub> corresponding to the first (upper) voltage plateau during discharge, and the HS Fe<sup>2+</sup>N<sub>6</sub>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>N<sub>6</sub> corresponding to the lower voltage plateau.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0031_R_d51652459e1101" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">24</a></sup></span> It is worth noting that FeHCF also displays similar behavior with two redox couples in the CV curve for Fe<sup>LS</sup> (3.31/3.58 V) and Fe<sup>HS</sup> (2.69/3.01 V); however, it shows larger peak separations presumably suggesting poorer electronic conductivity of FeHCF.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0022_R_d51652459e1109" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">15</a></sup></span> In the case of NiHCF, only one redox pair is observed, which can be assigned to Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> (LS), confirming Ni<sup>2+</sup> as redox-inactive species.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0017_R_d51652459e1119" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Mater. Chem. A">10</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="asia202301145-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2a9e4a56-b8b3-4933-8817-bda32c67ae8a/asia202301145-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2a9e4a56-b8b3-4933-8817-bda32c67ae8a/asia202301145-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b7bfb5d6-ef48-4877-bc32-d270c94b949f/asia202301145-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2a9e4a56-b8b3-4933-8817-bda32c67ae8a/asia202301145-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=asia202301145-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fasia.202301145" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Cyclic voltammograms of FeNiHCF, NiHCF, and FeHCF at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> between 2.0 and 4.2 V. (b) Galvanostatic voltage profiles of FeNiHCF, NiHCF, and FeHCF at a rate of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. (c) Charge-discharge profiles of FeNiHCF at current densities of 10 to 25600 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. (d) Capacity retention of FeNiHCF at various rates. (e) Rate capability plot of FeNiHCF.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>As shown in Figure <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0002">2b</a>, at a rate of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, FeNiHCF delivers a capacity of 132 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, significantly higher than those of FeHCF and NiHCF (94 and 86 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively). This may be explained by the fact that Ni-doping can help increase electronic conductivity (abundant d electrons of d<sup>8</sup> Ni<sup>2+</sup>), thus resulting in more facile Na-ion migration and ultimately enhancing the redox activity of both Fe<sup>HS</sup> and Fe<sup>LS</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0022_R_d51652459e1172" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">15</a></sup></span> The Ni substitution of Fe<sup>HS</sup> has been proved to promote an inductive effect, due to the electronegativity which could cause the shift of the electron cloud on the Fe<sup>LS</sup>–C bond toward the CN ligand (resulting in a subtle FT-IR redshift at ~600 cm<sup>−1</sup>).<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0023_R_d51652459e1182" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Mater. Chem. A">16</a></sup></span> This shift of electron cloud is equivalent to positioning this Fe<sup>LS</sup> to a more positive valence state, thus enhancing its electrochemical activity.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0023_R_d51652459e1187" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Mater. Chem. A">16</a></sup></span> As seen in Figure <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0002">2b</a>, with 10 % of Ni-doping in FeNiHCF, the capacity contribution from Fe<sup>LS</sup> is increased by ~50 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which is comparable to doped materials reported previously.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0023_R_d51652459e1198" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Mater. Chem. A">16</a></sup></span> Moreover, it has been reported that the enhanced performance of Fe<sup>LS</sup> could be attributed to the weaker crystal field splitting experienced by the C-coordinated Fe<sup>LS</sup> after Ni doping, resulting in more facile electron transfer between e<sub>g</sub> and t<sub>2g</sub> orbitals during redox processes.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0023_R_d51652459e1210" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Mater. Chem. A">16</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>To validate this observation, the values of conductivity were determined by the four-point probe method. It was found that the electronic conductivity of FeNiHCF is one order of magnitude higher than the other two compounds (1.7×10<sup>−6</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup> for FeNiHCF vs. 8.9×10<sup>−7</sup> and 1.4×10<sup>−7</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup> for FeHCF and NiHCF, respectively). The capacity retention of the three materials in Figure S3 indicates that FeNiHCF is superior to FeHCF and NiHCF at both low (100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>) and high rates (25600 mA g<sup>−1</sup>). The Coulombic efficiency plot of FeNiHCF (Figure S4) appears to be stable, while the undoped material FeHCF has a more fluctuating plot, suggesting relatively high instability of the FeHCF structure at such high cycling rate. Therefore, this confirms that Ni-doping, in fact, helps enhance the redox activity and electronic conductivity, as well as stabilizes the structure to a certain extent. As a result, the specific capacity, rate performance, and cycling stability may be greatly improved. It is noteworthy that the degree of Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancies and water contents may also play a role in the overall performance of the materials.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0010_R_d51652459e1232" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>FeNiHCF was also investigated at other cycling rates from 10 to 25600 mA g<sup>−1</sup> as shown in Figure <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0002">2c</a> and <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0002">2d</a>. At 10 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, FeNiHCF delivers an initial capacity as high as 148 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> which accounts for ~98 % of the theoretical capacity (after deducting the capacity contribution from the conductive carbon). At the current densities of 100, 400, 1600, 3200, and 6400 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, discharge capacities of 132, 122, 118, 112, and 102 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can be obtained with capacity retention of 93, 93, 90, 88, and 89 %, respectively, after 100 cycles. At extremely high rates of 12800, 19600, and 25600 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, reversible capacities of 73, 57, and 55 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can still be delivered after 1000 cycles, demonstrating excellent long-term cycling stability. The rate capability was also tested (Figure <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0002">2e</a>). By varying the current density from 100 to 38400 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, and back to 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, a highly reversible rate capability curve was obtained with relatively high Coulombic efficiency. Moreover, a capacity retention of 99 % was observed when the rate of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> was reapplied to the cell. This is attributed to the facile charge transfer kinetics of FeNiHCF and Na ions in the Ni-stabilized framework. The electrochemical performance observed in FeNiHCF is considered to be one of the best among all metal-doped PBAs, especially in terms of rate capability and specific capacity (Table S7).</p>
         
         <p>To elucidate the redox mechanism of FeNiHCF cathode in SIBs, <i>ex situ</i> XPS was carried out on the electrodes at different voltage positions during the first cycle. As shown in Figure S6, at open circuit voltage (OCV, ~3.0 V), the deconvolution of the pristine electrode reveals that all the Fe ions in FeNiHCF are Fe<sup>2+</sup> (Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub> observed at 708.5 and 721.4 eV, respectively).<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0019_R_d51652459e1279" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem. Eng. J.">12</a>, <a href="#asia202301145-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0024_R_d51652459e1279" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a></sup></span> Once the cell is charged up to 3.25 V, a small amount of Fe<sup>2+</sup> is oxidized to Fe<sup>3+</sup> (Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub> observed at 712.0 and 724.6 eV, respectively) as some Na<sup>+</sup> is removed from the FeNiHCF lattice in order to maintain charge neutrality.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0019_R_d51652459e1293" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem. Eng. J.">12</a>, <a href="#asia202301145-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0024_R_d51652459e1293" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a></sup></span> On further charge up to 4.2 V, more Fe becomes Fe<sup>3+</sup> (with mostly Fe<sup>LS</sup> being active) to charge balance the additional removal of Na<sup>+</sup> (as shown in Figure S6c). When the cell is discharged to 3.25 and finally to 2.0 V, it is obvious that the amount of Fe<sup>3+</sup> decreases while Fe<sup>2+</sup> concentration increases. For Ni 2p XPS spectra (Figure S7), it is apparent that starting from pristine to the end of charge (4.2 V) and back to the end of discharge (2.0 V), Ni ions remain as Ni<sup>2+</sup> (Ni 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Ni 2p<sub>1/2</sub> observed at 856.6 and 874.2 eV, respectively) confirming that Ni is electrochemically inactive throughout the cell cycling process.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0022_R_d51652459e1314" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">15</a>, <a href="#asia202301145-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0030_R_d51652459e1314" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Chem. Eng. J.">23</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>To gain insight into the structural evolution of FeNiHCF, <i>in situ</i> synchrotron X-ray diffraction was performed on the Na/FeNiHCF cell cycled at a current density of 40 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in Figure <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0003">3b</a>, when the cell is charged (shown in black), the major peaks of FeNiHCF at 17.4, 24.6, and 35.0° corresponding to (200), (220), and (400) reflections are obviously shifted to higher 2θ. This indicates that the unit cell parameters become smaller as Na<sup>+</sup> is extracted from the PBA lattice and Fe<sup>2+</sup> is oxidized.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0023_R_d51652459e1332" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Mater. Chem. A">16</a>, <a href="#asia202301145-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0026_R_d51652459e1332" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Power Sources">19</a></sup></span> During discharge (shown in red), the peaks are shifted back toward a lower 2θ, indicating a reversible change of the structure. Le Bail refinements were carried out on the <i>in situ</i> synchrotron X-ray diffraction data to determine the change in lattice parameters of FeNiHCF during the charge-discharge process. The results in Figure <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0003">3c</a> and S8 reveal that the unit cell slightly contracts during charge and expands during discharge. It is worth noting that the change in lattice parameter <i>a</i> is extremely small (~1 %) during cycling. Such small volume change and suppressed structural distortion, enabled by the Ni substitution, can also be witnessed in SEM images in Figure S9. After 100 cycles, the FeNiHCF particles in the SIB electrodes appear relatively intact without any obvious signs of deformation or degradation.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="asia202301145-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4386631e-2c56-4279-9853-f417622eb027/asia202301145-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4386631e-2c56-4279-9853-f417622eb027/asia202301145-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9f23693e-c373-429b-a33d-50b868f09d0a/asia202301145-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4386631e-2c56-4279-9853-f417622eb027/asia202301145-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=asia202301145-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fasia.202301145" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p><i>In situ</i> synchrotron X-ray diffraction of Na/FeNiHCF cell: (a) charge-discharge profile between 2.0 and 4.2 V at a rate of 40 mA g<sup>−1</sup>; (b) diffraction patterns highlighting the change of the major peaks (with a peak at 26.5° from MGL carbon substrate); and (c) the change in lattice parameter <i>a</i> of FeNiHCF (shown in red) during the charge-discharge process (shown in black).</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To obtain better understanding on the charge transport kinetics of the Ni-doped material FeNiHCF, CV was carried out at various scan rates from 0.1 to 1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0004">4a</a>). It can be observed that the redox process has relatively low polarization as the scan rate increases, suggesting fast charge transfer. The Na-ion diffusion coefficient <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/53decb03-b28d-4aaa-8085-fdae71b07137/asia202301145-math-0002.png"></span><math></math>
 can be calculated from the Randles−Sevcik equation: <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/6dd23ffd-aaab-405e-bdf5-01dc0cba1b15/asia202301145-math-0003.png"></span><math></math>
 where <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/3941965a-96a5-4544-85b9-e0a2136e6947/asia202301145-math-0004.png"></span><math></math>
 is the peak current (A), <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/fa9a03e8-bf5a-4260-962c-935c27c57fef/asia202301145-math-0005.png"></span><math></math>
 is the number of electrons per formula unit, <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/01d4dfa1-0e3d-4698-b5bd-0dc67ca1bc9a/asia202301145-math-0006.png"></span><math></math>
 is the area of the electrode (1.1 cm<sup>2</sup>), <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/f453b375-864b-42ea-8ddb-b0a5033c46a3/asia202301145-math-0007.png"></span><math></math>
 is the concentration of Na<sup>+</sup> in the electrolyte (10<sup>−3</sup> mol cm<sup>−3</sup>), and <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/ac58bd35-535d-4d01-8400-50dd7761ea0f/asia202301145-math-0008.png"></span><math></math>
 is the scan rate (V s<sup>−1</sup>). The peak current is proportional to the square root of the scan rate, and the diffusion coefficient can be determined from the slope of the linear plot as shown in Figure <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0004">4b</a>.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0032_R_d51652459e1523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25">25</a></sup></span> The calculated diffusion coefficients <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/b31fe849-6dd8-4cde-a714-4f265ed6ea3f/asia202301145-math-0009.png"></span><math></math>
 are in the range of 10<sup>−10</sup>–10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> (Table S3).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="asia202301145-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c3816887-650a-40be-8fd3-285f79233d3d/asia202301145-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c3816887-650a-40be-8fd3-285f79233d3d/asia202301145-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9227c491-58f1-4e14-9ab8-1ed72739a65e/asia202301145-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c3816887-650a-40be-8fd3-285f79233d3d/asia202301145-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=asia202301145-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fasia.202301145" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) CV curves of FeNiHCF at various scan rates from 0.1 to 1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. (b) Relationship between the peak current <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/5a931df7-ed3c-44ec-be89-13a6c633fc92/asia202301145-math-0010.png"></span><math></math>
 and square root of scan rate v<sup>1/2</sup> for determining Na-ion diffusion coefficients. (c) Relationship between log (peak current, <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/bf61e3b6-e8eb-4ebe-b922-bb83173e06d4/asia202301145-math-0011.png"></span><math></math>
) and log (scan rate, v) from the CV curves in (a) for determining the <i>b</i> values. (d) and (e) Capacity contribution at different scan rates for peak A (Fe<sup>HS</sup>) and peak B (Fe<sup>LS</sup>), respectively.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Additional analysis of the charge storage process was performed on FeNiHCF according to the relationship between log <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/b8b75b60-4269-4107-96dd-0dbe7946cb18/asia202301145-math-0012.png"></span><math></math>
 and log <i>v</i> from the CV curves in Figure <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0004">4a</a>. The measured current <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/f9e3518c-8dd2-4cd6-9e4f-cbd9dd3fad70/asia202301145-math-0013.png"></span><math></math>
 is fit to a power law <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/ca5f9f5d-38fd-4470-b1c1-fe72d220351a/asia202301145-math-0014.png"></span><math></math>
with scan rate <i>v</i>, and <i>b</i> can be determined from the slope of the log <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/e2a8e7a7-a2a1-4e8a-bec9-d4b11ab0116d/asia202301145-math-0015.png"></span><math></math>
 vs. log <i>v</i> as listed in Figure <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0004">4c</a>.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0035_R_d51652459e1689" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26">26</a></sup></span> For peaks A and D which correspond to the Fe<sup>HS</sup> sites, the analysis shows that the Faradaic charge storage associated with the peak maxima in CV has a more significant diffusion contribution (<i>b</i>=0.83 and 0.72, respectively); therefore, it is largely diffusion-controlled charge storage mechanism (<i>b</i>=0.5 indicating traditional diffusion dominated charge storage, while <i>b</i>=1 indicating capacitor-like charge storage).<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0035_R_d51652459e1701" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26">26</a></sup></span> In case of peaks B and C which are attributed to Fe<sup>LS</sup> sites, the <i>b</i> values (0.91 and 0.93, respectively) are much closer to 1, indicating that the charge storage on the Fe<sup>LS</sup> sites is predominantly non-diffusion limited process. This phonomenon has also been observed previously where the redox reaction of Fe<sup>LS</sup> is known to preserve the crystal structure with outstanding kinetics and cycle life, while the HS metal exhibits poorer kinetics due to possible structural distortions arising from the weak N-coordinated crystal field.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0010_R_d51652459e1713" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a>, <a href="#asia202301145-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0023_R_d51652459e1713" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Mater. Chem. A">16</a>, <a href="#asia202301145-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0038_R_d51652459e1713" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">27</a></sup></span> Consistently, the discharge curves of FeNiHCF in Figure <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0002">2c</a> reveal that the high rate capability of the Ni-doped material is mostly contributed by the LS Fe<sup>2+</sup>C<sub>6</sub>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>C<sub>6</sub> redox couple.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0023_R_d51652459e1728" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Mater. Chem. A">16</a></sup></span> As the rate increases, the capacity loss from the HS Fe<sup>2+</sup>N<sub>6</sub>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>N<sub>6</sub> redox couple at the lower discharge plateau is known to result from cubic-rhombohedral phase transition which tends to be irreversible, especially at higher charge-discharge rates.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0023_R_d51652459e1740" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Mater. Chem. A">16</a>, <a href="#asia202301145-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0038_R_d51652459e1740" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">27</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>To further quantitatively differentiate the contribution of the capacitive and diffusion-controlled elements to the overall capacity, the relationship <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/1fd0b546-0501-4bea-a7e7-7bd9b300416f/asia202301145-math-0016.png"></span><math></math>
can be divided into two terms: the capacitive effects (<i>k</i><sub>1</sub>v) and diffusion-limited effects (<i>k</i><sub>2</sub>v<sup>1/2</sup>) as follows: <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/4c696b4c-b7ab-4a81-bedb-f141077e2732/asia202301145-math-0017.png"></span><math></math>
=<i>k</i><sub>1</sub>v + <i>k</i><sub>2</sub>v<sup>1/2</sup>. By plotting v<sup>1/2</sup> vs. <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/2687c0a5-7f33-4575-9059-0a00c4131433/asia202301145-math-0018.png"></span><math></math>
 /v<sup>1/2</sup>, <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> can be derived from the slope and the y-axis intercept, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#asia202301145-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia202301145-bib-0039_R_d51652459e1821" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28">28</a></sup></span> As shown in Figures <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0004">4d</a> and <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0004">4e</a>, overall the capacitive contribution seems to be more dominating in Fe<sup>LS</sup> sites (peak B, Figure <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0004">4e</a>) compared to Fe<sup>HS</sup> sites (peak A, Figure <a href="#asia202301145-fig-0004">4d</a>), and it is obvious that the ratio of stored charge contributed by the capacitive process increases with the scan rate, therefore confirming an excellent rate performance of FeNiHCF cathode.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="asia202301145-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="asia202301145-sec-0003-title"> Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>FeNiHCF has been prepared and electrochemically investigated in comparison with FeHCF and NiHCF as cathode in SIBs. The Ni-doping at the HS sites has been found to increase the electronic conductivity by an order of magnitude, due to the abundant d-orbital electrons of d<sup>8</sup> Ni(II), thereby enhancing the overall redox activity of both redox centers, Fe<sup>HS</sup> and Fe<sup>LS</sup>. Additional performance enhancement (at Fe<sup>LS</sup> specifically) arises from the inductive effect and weaker crystal field splitting experienced by Fe<sup>LS</sup> after Ni substitution, resulting in more electrochemical activity and more facile electron/ion transport during redox processes. The presence of Ni in FeNiHCF also suppresses volume change and structural distortion as witnessed in the very small change in lattice parameters (~1 %). As a result, high structural stability and long-term cyclability of PBA-based SIBs can be achieved. HS Fe<sup>2+</sup>N<sub>6</sub>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>N<sub>6</sub> redox couple has been shown to be kinetically sluggish during cycling with large possibility of structural distortion or phase transition (due to the weak N-coordinated crystal field), which tends to be irreversible at high rates, leading to capacity loss. On the other hand, LS Fe<sup>2+</sup>C<sub>6</sub>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>C<sub>6</sub> redox couple has been observed to have outstanding kinetics with a dominating capacitive contribution. The excellent high rate capacity of FeNiHCF is found to be primarily contributed by Fe<sup>LS</sup>. Therefore, as witnessed in this work, activating Fe<sup>LS</sup> by Ni doping in FeNiHCF can greatly improve the specific capacity, rate performance as well as long-term cycling stability, resulting in one of the best performances ever reported for metal-doped PBAs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="asia202301145-sec-0004">
